<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Prejudice or discrimination based on a person's sex or gender</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Sex discrimination" redirects here. For discrimination based on sexuality, see <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation_discrimination" title="Sexual orientation discrimination">Sexual orientation discrimination</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Anti-sexism" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Anti-sexism_(disambiguation)" title="Anti-sexism (disambiguation)">Anti-sexism (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Discrimination_sidebar" title="Template:Discrimination sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Discrimination_sidebar" title="Template talk:Discrimination sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Discrimination_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Sexism</b> is <a href="/wiki/Prejudice" title="Prejudice">prejudice</a> or <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a> based on one's <a href="/wiki/Sex" title="Sex">sex</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a>. Sexism can affect anyone, but it primarily affects <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Women" title="Women">women</a> and <a href="/wiki/Girl" title="Girl">girls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sexism_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sexism-1">[1]</a></sup> It has been linked to <a href="/wiki/Stereotype" title="Stereotype">stereotypes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gender_role" title="Gender role">gender roles</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and may include the belief that one sex or gender is intrinsically superior to another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">[4]</a></sup> Extreme sexism may foster <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a>, and other forms of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_violence" title="Sexual violence">sexual violence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> <b>Gender discrimination</b> may encompass sexism. This term is defined as discrimination toward people based on their <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macklem_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macklem-6">[6]</a></sup> or their gender or sex differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lenhart_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lenhart-7">[7]</a></sup> Gender discrimination is especially defined in terms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Workplace_inequality" title="Workplace inequality">workplace inequality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lenhart_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lenhart-7">[7]</a></sup> It may arise from social or cultural customs and norms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_definitions">Etymology and definitions</span></h2>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Fred_R._Shapiro" title="Fred R. Shapiro">Fred R. Shapiro</a>, the term "sexism" was most likely coined on November 18, 1965, by Pauline M. Leet during a "Student-Faculty Forum" at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franklin_and_Marshall_College" title="Franklin and Marshall College">Franklin and Marshall College</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wordpress2007_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-wordpress2007-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Self-published_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on a self-published source. (June 2020)">self-published source?</span></a></i>]</sup> Specifically, the word sexism appears in Leet's forum contribution "Women and the Undergraduate", and she defines it by comparing it to racism, stating in part (on page 3): "When you argue ... that since fewer women write good poetry this justifies their total exclusion, you are taking a position analogous to that of the racist—I might call you, in this case, a 'sexist' ... Both the racist and the sexist are acting as if all that has happened had never happened, and both of them are making decisions and coming to conclusions about someone's value by referring to factors which are in both cases irrelevant."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wordpress2007_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-wordpress2007-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also, according to Shapiro, the first time the term "sexism" appeared in print was in <a href="/wiki/Caroline_Bird_(American_author)" title="Caroline Bird (American author)">Caroline Bird</a>'s speech "On Being Born Female", which was published on November 15, 1968, in <i>Vital Speeches of the Day</i> (p. 6).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wordpress2007_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-wordpress2007-9">[9]</a></sup> In this speech she said in part: "There is recognition abroad that we are in many ways a sexist country. Sexism is judging people by their sex when sex doesn't matter. Sexism is intended to rhyme with racism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wordpress2007_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-wordpress2007-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sexism may be defined as an <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a> based on the belief that one sex is superior to another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaefer_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaefer-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-13">[13]</a></sup> It is discrimination, prejudice, or stereotyping based on gender, and is most often expressed toward women and girls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sexism_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sexism-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">Sociology</a> has examined sexism as manifesting at both the individual and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_institution" title="Social institution">institutional</a> level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaefer_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schaefer-11">[11]</a></sup> According to Richard Schaefer, sexism is perpetuated by all major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_institutions" title="Social institutions">social institutions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaefer_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schaefer-11">[11]</a></sup> Sociologists describe parallels among other ideological systems of oppression such as <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a>, which also operates at both the individual and institutional level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Early female sociologists <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Perkins_Gilman" title="Charlotte Perkins Gilman">Charlotte Perkins Gilman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ida_B._Wells" title="Ida B. Wells">Ida B. Wells</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Martineau" title="Harriet Martineau">Harriet Martineau</a> described systems of <a href="/wiki/Gender_inequality" title="Gender inequality">gender inequality</a>, but did not use the term <i>sexism</i>, which was coined later. Sociologists who adopted the functionalist paradigm, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Talcott_Parsons" title="Talcott Parsons">Talcott Parsons</a>, understood gender inequality as the natural outcome of a dimorphic model of gender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Psychologist" title="Psychologist">Psychologists</a> Mary Crawford and <a href="/wiki/Rhoda_Unger" title="Rhoda Unger">Rhoda Unger</a> define sexism as prejudice held by individuals that encompasses "negative attitudes and values about women as a group."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Peter Glick and <a href="/wiki/Susan_Fiske" title="Susan Fiske">Susan Fiske</a> coined the term <i><a href="/wiki/Ambivalent_sexism" title="Ambivalent sexism">ambivalent sexism</a></i> to describe how stereotypes about women can be both positive and negative, and that individuals compartmentalize the stereotypes they hold into hostile sexism or benevolent sexism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Feminist_theory" title="Feminist theory">Feminist</a> author <a href="/wiki/Bell_hooks" title="Bell hooks">bell hooks</a> defines sexism as a system of oppression that results in disadvantages for women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Feminist philosopher <a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Frye" title="Marilyn Frye">Marilyn Frye</a> defines sexism as an "attitudinal-conceptual-cognitive-orientational complex" of <a href="/wiki/Androcentrism" title="Androcentrism">male supremacy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Male_chauvinism" title="Male chauvinism">male chauvinism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Misogyny" title="Misogyny">misogyny</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">Philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/Kate_Manne" title="Kate Manne">Kate Manne</a> defines sexism as one branch of a <a href="/wiki/Patriarchy" title="Patriarchy">patriarchal order</a>. In her definition, sexism rationalizes and justifies patriarchal norms, in contrast with <a href="/wiki/Misogyny" title="Misogyny">misogyny</a>, the branch which polices and enforces patriarchal norms. Manne says that sexism often attempts to make patriarchal social arrangements seem natural, good, or inevitable so that there appears to be no reason to resist them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History"><span class="anchor" id="History_of_gender_discrimination"></span> History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_world"><span class="anchor" id="Ancient_sexism"></span> Ancient world</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Hindoo_Widow_Burning_Herself_with_the_Corpse_of_her_Husband.jpg"><img alt="Engraving of a woman preparing to self-immolate with her husband's corpse" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="465" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/A_Hindoo_Widow_Burning_Herself_with_the_Corpse_of_her_Husband.jpg/220px-A_Hindoo_Widow_Burning_Herself_with_the_Corpse_of_her_Husband.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/A_Hindoo_Widow_Burning_Herself_with_the_Corpse_of_her_Husband.jpg/330px-A_Hindoo_Widow_Burning_Herself_with_the_Corpse_of_her_Husband.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/A_Hindoo_Widow_Burning_Herself_with_the_Corpse_of_her_Husband.jpg/440px-A_Hindoo_Widow_Burning_Herself_with_the_Corpse_of_her_Husband.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Hindoo_Widow_Burning_Herself_with_the_Corpse_of_her_Husband.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sati_(practice)" title="Sati (practice)">Sati</a>, or self-immolation by widows, was prevalent in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> society until the early 19th century.</div></div></div>
<p>The status of <a href="/wiki/Women_in_ancient_Egypt" title="Women in ancient Egypt">women in ancient Egypt</a> depended on their fathers or husbands, but they had property rights and could attend court, including as plaintiffs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silverman_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silverman-21">[21]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_women" title="Anglo-Saxon women">Women of the Anglo-Saxon era</a> were commonly afforded equal status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lehmberg_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lehmberg-22">[22]</a></sup> Evidence, however, is lacking to support the idea that many pre-agricultural societies afforded women a higher status than women today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lockard_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lockard-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> After the adoption of agriculture and sedentary cultures, the concept that one gender was inferior to the other was established; most often this was imposed upon women and girls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Examples of unequal treatment of women in the ancient world include written laws preventing women from participating in the political process; <a href="/wiki/Women_in_ancient_Rome" title="Women in ancient Rome">women in ancient Rome</a> could not vote or hold <a href="/wiki/Roman_magistrate" title="Roman magistrate">political office</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Another example is scholarly texts that indoctrinate children in female inferiority; <a href="/wiki/Women_in_ancient_and_imperial_China" title="Women in ancient and imperial China">women in ancient China</a> were taught the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian" title="Confucian">Confucian</a> principles that a woman should <a href="/wiki/Three_Obediences_and_Four_Virtues" title="Three Obediences and Four Virtues">obey</a> her father in childhood, husband in marriage, and son in widowhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wu_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-wu-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Witch_hunts_and_trials">Witch hunts and trials</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Witch_hunt" title="Witch hunt">Witch hunt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malleus_maleficarum,_K%C3%B6ln_1520,_Titelseite.jpg"><img alt="Titlepage from the book Malleus Maleficarum" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="455" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Malleus_maleficarum%2C_K%C3%B6ln_1520%2C_Titelseite.jpg/260px-Malleus_maleficarum%2C_K%C3%B6ln_1520%2C_Titelseite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Malleus_maleficarum%2C_K%C3%B6ln_1520%2C_Titelseite.jpg/390px-Malleus_maleficarum%2C_K%C3%B6ln_1520%2C_Titelseite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/Malleus_maleficarum%2C_K%C3%B6ln_1520%2C_Titelseite.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malleus_maleficarum,_K%C3%B6ln_1520,_Titelseite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The Hammer of Witches which destroyeth Witches and their heresy as with a two-edged sword". Title page of the seventh <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> edition of the <i><a href="/wiki/Malleus_Maleficarum" title="Malleus Maleficarum">Malleus Maleficarum</a></i>, 1520, from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sydney_Library" title="University of Sydney Library">University of Sydney Library</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Sexism may have been the impetus that fueled the witch trials between the 15th and 18th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Europe" title="Early modern Europe">early modern Europe</a>, and in the European colonies in North America, claims were made that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Witch" title="Witch">witches</a> were a threat to <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>. The misogyny of that period played a role in the persecution of these women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Malleus_Maleficarum" title="Malleus Maleficarum">Malleus Maleficarum</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Kramer" title="Heinrich Kramer">Heinrich Kramer</a>, the book which played a major role in the witch hunts and trials, the author argues that women are more likely to practice witchcraft than men, and writes that:
</p>
<dl><dd>All wickedness is but little to the wickedness of a woman ... What else is a woman but a foe to friendship, an inescapable punishment, a necessary evil, a natural temptation, a desirable calamity, a domestic danger, a delectable detriment, an evil of nature, painted with fair colors!<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Witchcraft remains illegal in several countries, including <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, where it is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punishable_by_death" title="Punishable by death">punishable by death</a>. In 2011, a woman was beheaded in that country for "witchcraft and sorcery".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Murders of women after being accused of witchcraft remain common in some parts of the world; for example, in <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, about 500 elderly women are murdered each year following such accusations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>When women are targeted with accusations of witchcraft and subsequent violence, it is often the case that several forms of discrimination interact – for example, discrimination based on gender with discrimination based on <a href="/wiki/Caste" title="Caste">caste</a>, as is the case in India and Nepal, where such crimes are relatively common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coverture_and_other_marriage_regulations">Coverture and other marriage regulations</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Coverture" title="Coverture">Coverture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marital_power" title="Marital power">Marital power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Restitution_of_conjugal_rights" title="Restitution of conjugal rights">Restitution of conjugal rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kirchberg_v._Feenstra" title="Kirchberg v. Feenstra">Kirchberg v. Feenstra</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marriage_bar" title="Marriage bar">Marriage bar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Say_no_to_dowry.jpg"><img alt="An Indian Anti-dowry poster headed Say No To Dowry" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2511" data-file-width="1613" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Say_no_to_dowry.jpg/170px-Say_no_to_dowry.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Say_no_to_dowry.jpg/255px-Say_no_to_dowry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Say_no_to_dowry.jpg/340px-Say_no_to_dowry.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Say_no_to_dowry.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anti-<a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a> poster in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bangalore,_India" title="Bangalore, India">Bangalore, India</a>. According to Amnesty International, "[T]he ongoing reality of dowry-related violence is an example of what can happen when women are treated as property."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Until the 20th century, U.S. and <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English law</a> observed the system of <a href="/wiki/Coverture" title="Coverture">coverture</a>, where "by marriage, the husband and wife are one person in law; that is the very being or legal existence of the woman is suspended during the marriage".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blackstone_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-blackstone-38">[38]</a></sup> U.S. women were not legally defined as "persons" until 1875 (<i><a href="/wiki/Minor_v._Happersett" title="Minor v. Happersett">Minor v. Happersett</a></i>, 88 U.S. 162).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-legacy98.org_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-legacy98.org-39">[39]</a></sup> A similar legal doctrine, called <a href="/wiki/Marital_power" title="Marital power">marital power</a>, existed under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Dutch_law" title="Roman Dutch law">Roman Dutch law</a> (and is still partially in force in present-day <a href="/wiki/Eswatini" title="Eswatini">Eswatini</a>).
</p><p>Restrictions on married women's rights were common in Western countries until a few decades ago: for instance, French married women obtained the right to work without their husband's permission in 1965,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and in West Germany women obtained this right in 1977.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Franco</a> era, in Spain, a married woman required her husband's consent (called <i>permiso marital</i>) for employment, ownership of property and traveling away from home; the <i>permiso marital</i> was abolished in 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In Australia, until 1983, a married woman's passport application had to be authorized by her husband.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Women in parts of the world continue to lose their legal rights in marriage. For example, <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemeni</a> marriage regulations state that a wife must obey her husband and must not leave home without his permission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, the law allows husbands to legally "punish" their wives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a>, the Family Code states that the husband is the head of the household; the wife owes her obedience to her husband; a wife has to live with her husband wherever he chooses to live; and wives must have their husbands' authorization to bring a case in court or initiate other legal proceedings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Abuses and discriminatory practices against women in marriage are often rooted in financial payments such as <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">bride price</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dower" title="Dower">dower</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> These transactions often serve as legitimizing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coercive_control" title="Coercive control">coercive control</a> of the wife by her husband and in giving him authority over her; for instance Article 13 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_of_Personal_Status_(Tunisia)" title="Code of Personal Status (Tunisia)">Code of Personal Status (Tunisia)</a> states that, "The husband shall not, in default of payment of the dower, force the woman to consummate the marriage",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> implying that, if the dower is paid, <a href="/wiki/Marital_rape" title="Marital rape">marital rape</a> is permitted. In this regard, critics have questioned the alleged gains of women in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, and its image as a progressive country in the region, arguing that discrimination against women remains very strong there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Organisation_Against_Torture" title="World Organisation Against Torture">World Organisation Against Torture</a> (OMCT) has recognized the "independence and ability to leave an abusive husband" as crucial in stopping mistreatment of women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> However, in some parts of the world, once married, women have very little chance of leaving a violent husband: obtaining a <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">divorce</a> is very difficult in many jurisdictions because of the need to prove <a href="/wiki/Divorce#At-fault_divorce" title="Divorce">fault</a> in court. While attempting a <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> separation (moving away from the marital home) is also impossible because of laws preventing this. For instance, in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, a wife who leaves her marital home risks being imprisoned for "running away".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In addition, many former British colonies, including <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, maintain the concept of <a href="/wiki/Restitution_of_conjugal_rights" title="Restitution of conjugal rights">restitution of conjugal rights</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> under which a wife may be ordered by court to return to her husband; if she fails to do so, she may be held in <a href="/wiki/Contempt_of_court" title="Contempt of court">contempt of court</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Other problems have to do with the payment of the <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">bride price</a>: if the wife wants to leave, her husband may demand the return of the bride price that he had paid to the woman's family; and the woman's family often cannot or does not want to pay it back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Laws, regulations, and traditions related to marriage continue to discriminate against women in many parts of the world, and to contribute to the mistreatment of women, in particular in areas related to <a href="/wiki/Sexual_violence" title="Sexual violence">sexual violence</a> and to self-determination regarding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexuality" title="Sexuality">sexuality</a>, the violation of the latter now being acknowledged as a violation of <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a>. In 2012, <a href="/wiki/Navi_Pillay" title="Navi Pillay">Navi Pillay</a>, then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Commissioner_for_Human_Rights" title="High Commissioner for Human Rights">High Commissioner for Human Rights</a>, stated that:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Women are frequently treated as property, they are sold into marriage, into trafficking, into sexual slavery. Violence against women frequently takes the form of sexual violence. Victims of such violence are often accused of promiscuity and held responsible for their fate, while infertile women are rejected by husbands, families and communities. In many countries, married women may not refuse to have sexual relations with their husbands, and often have no say in whether they use contraception<span class="nowrap"> </span>... Ensuring that women have full autonomy over their bodies is the first crucial step towards achieving substantive equality between women and men. Personal issues—such as when, how and with whom they choose to have sex, and when, how and with whom they choose to have children—are at the heart of living a life in dignity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suffrage_and_politics">Suffrage and politics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annie_Kenney_and_Christabel_Pankhurst.jpg"><img alt='Two woman carry a sign reading "Votes for Women".' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1077" data-file-width="717" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Annie_Kenney_and_Christabel_Pankhurst.jpg/170px-Annie_Kenney_and_Christabel_Pankhurst.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Annie_Kenney_and_Christabel_Pankhurst.jpg/255px-Annie_Kenney_and_Christabel_Pankhurst.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Annie_Kenney_and_Christabel_Pankhurst.jpg/340px-Annie_Kenney_and_Christabel_Pankhurst.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Annie_Kenney_and_Christabel_Pankhurst.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Annie_Kenney" title="Annie Kenney">Annie Kenney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christabel_Pankhurst" title="Christabel Pankhurst">Christabel Pankhurst</a></div></div></div>
<p>Gender has been used as a tool for discrimination against women in the political sphere. <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">Women's suffrage</a> was not achieved until 1893, when New Zealand was the first country to grant women the right to vote. Saudi Arabia is the most recent country, as of August 2015, to extend the right to vote to women in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Some Western countries allowed women the right to vote only relatively recently. <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss</a> women gained the right to vote in federal elections in 1971,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-switzerland-chronology_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-switzerland-chronology-67">[67]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Appenzell_Innerrhoden" title="Appenzell Innerrhoden">Appenzell Innerrhoden</a> became the last <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_Switzerland" title="Cantons of Switzerland">canton</a> to grant women the right to vote on local issues in 1991, when it was forced to do so by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Supreme_Court_of_Switzerland" title="Federal Supreme Court of Switzerland">Federal Supreme Court of Switzerland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> French women were granted the right to vote in 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In Greece, women obtained the right to vote in 1952.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, women obtained the right to vote in 1984, through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_women%27s_suffrage_referendum,_1984" title="Liechtenstein women's suffrage referendum, 1984">women's suffrage referendum of 1984</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>While almost every woman today has the right to vote, there is still progress to be made for women in politics. Studies have shown that in several democracies including Australia, Canada, and the United States, women are still represented using gender stereotypes in the press.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KittilsonFridkin2008_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-KittilsonFridkin2008-74">[74]</a></sup> Multiple authors have shown that gender differences in the media are less evident today than they used to be in the 1980s, but are still present. Certain issues (e.g., education) are likely to be linked with female candidates, while other issues (e.g., taxes) are likely to be linked with male candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KittilsonFridkin2008_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-KittilsonFridkin2008-74">[74]</a></sup> In addition, there is more emphasis on female candidates' personal qualities, such as their appearance and their personality, as females are portrayed as emotional and dependent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KittilsonFridkin2008_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-KittilsonFridkin2008-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a widespread imbalance of lawmaking power between men and women. The ratio of women to men in legislatures is used as a measure of gender equality in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations'</a> <a href="/wiki/Gender_Empowerment_Measure" title="Gender Empowerment Measure">Gender Empowerment Measure</a> and its newer incarnation the <a href="/wiki/Gender_Inequality_Index" title="Gender Inequality Index">Gender Inequality Index</a>. Speaking about China, Lanyan Chen stated that, since men more than women serve as the gatekeepers of policy making, this may lead to women's needs not being properly represented. In this sense, the inequality in lawmaking power also causes gender discrimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Menus">Menus</span></h4>
<p>Until the early 1980s, some high-end restaurants had two <a href="/wiki/Menu" title="Menu">menus</a>: a regular menu with the prices listed for men and a second menu for women, which did not have the prices listed (it was called the "ladies' menu"), so that the female diner would not know the prices of the items.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frost_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frost-76">[76]</a></sup> In 1980, Kathleen Bick took a male business partner out to dinner at L'Orangerie in West Hollywood. After she was given a women's menu without prices and her guest got one with prices, Bick hired lawyer <a href="/wiki/Gloria_Allred" title="Gloria Allred">Gloria Allred</a> to file a discrimination lawsuit, on the grounds that the women's menu went against the California Civil Rights Act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frost_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frost-76">[76]</a></sup> Bick stated that getting a women's menu without prices left her feeling "humiliated and incensed". The owners of the restaurant defended the practice, saying it was done as a courtesy, like the way men would stand up when a woman enters the room. Even though the lawsuit was dropped, the restaurant ended its gender-based menu policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frost_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Frost-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trends_over_time">Trends over time</span></h3>
<div class="boilerplate metadata plainlinks tfd" id="tfd" style="background-color: transparent; padding: 0; font-size:xx-small; color:#000000; text-align: center; border-bottom:1px solid #AAAAAA; overflow:hidden;">‹ The <a href="/wiki/Help:Template" title="Help:Template">template</a> below (<i><a href="/wiki/Template:Globalize" title="Template:Globalize">Globalize</a></i>) is being considered for merging. See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Templates_for_discussion/Log/2022_October_6#Template:Globalize" title="Wikipedia:Templates for discussion/Log/2022 October 6">templates for discussion</a> to help reach a consensus. ›</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Globalize plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Globe icon." data-file-height="290" data-file-width="350" decoding="async" height="40" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/97px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 2x" width="48"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The examples and perspective in this section <b>may not represent a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias">worldwide view</a> of the class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_stereotypes">Gender stereotypes</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gender_role#Gender_stereotypes" title="Gender role">Gender role § Gender stereotypes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Implicit_stereotype#Gender_stereotypes" title="Implicit stereotype">Implicit stereotype § Gender stereotypes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bettie_Page_driving.jpg"><img alt="Series of photographs lampooning women drivers" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1202" data-file-width="1758" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Bettie_Page_driving.jpg/300px-Bettie_Page_driving.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Bettie_Page_driving.jpg/450px-Bettie_Page_driving.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Bettie_Page_driving.jpg/600px-Bettie_Page_driving.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bettie_Page_driving.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bettie_Page" title="Bettie Page">Bettie Page</a> portrays stereotypes about women <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile_Driving" title="Automobile Driving">drivers</a> in 1952.</div></div></div>
<p>Gender stereotypes are widely held beliefs about the characteristics and behavior of women and men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical" title="Empirical">Empirical</a> studies have found widely shared cultural beliefs that men are more socially valued and more competent than women in a number of activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Dustin B. Thoman and others (2008) hypothesize that "[t]he socio-cultural salience of ability versus other components of the gender-math stereotype may impact women pursuing math". Through the experiment comparing the math outcomes of women under two various gender-math stereotype components, which are the ability of math and the effort on math respectively, Thoman and others found that women's math performance is more likely to be affected by the negative ability stereotype, which is influenced by sociocultural beliefs in the United States, rather than the effort component. As a result of this experiment and the sociocultural beliefs in the United States, Thoman and others concluded that individuals' academic outcomes can be affected by the gender-math stereotype component that is influenced by the sociocultural beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below">
<img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1048" data-file-width="648" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/17px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/26px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Woman-power_emblem.svg/34px-Woman-power_emblem.svg.png 2x" width="17"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Feminism" title="Portal:Feminism">Feminism portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Feminism_sidebar" title="Template:Feminism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Feminism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Feminism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Feminism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Sexism in language exists when language devalues members of a certain gender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online.santarosa.edu_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-online.santarosa.edu-82">[82]</a></sup> Sexist language, in many instances, promotes male superiority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Sexism in language affects consciousness, perceptions of reality, encoding and transmitting cultural meanings and socialization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-online.santarosa.edu_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-online.santarosa.edu-82">[82]</a></sup> Researchers have pointed to the semantic rule in operation in language of the <a href="/wiki/Male_as_norm" title="Male as norm">male-as-norm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marxists.org_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-marxists.org-84">[84]</a></sup> This results in sexism as the male becomes the standard and those who are not male are relegated to the inferior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marxists.org_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-marxists.org-84">[84]</a></sup> Sexism in language is considered a form of indirect sexism because it is not always overt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Examples include:
</p>
<ul><li>Using generic masculine terms to reference a group of mixed gender, such as "mankind", "man" (referring to humanity), "guys", or "officers and men"</li>
<li>Using the singular masculine pronoun (he, his, him) as the default to refer to a person of unknown gender</li>
<li>Terms ending in "-man" that may be performed by those of non-male genders, such as businessman, chairman, or policeman</li>
<li>Using unnecessary gender markers, such as "male nurse" implying that simply a "nurse" is by default assumed to be female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennison_&amp;_Trofe_2002_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kennison_&amp;_Trofe_2002-86">[86]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexist_and_gender-neutral_language">Sexist and gender-neutral language</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gender-neutral_language" title="Gender-neutral language">Gender-neutral language</a></div>
<p>Various 20th century feminist movements, from <a href="/wiki/Liberal_feminism" title="Liberal feminism">liberal feminism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radical_feminism" title="Radical feminism">radical feminism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Standpoint_feminism" title="Standpoint feminism">standpoint feminism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_feminism" title="Postmodern feminism">postmodern feminism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Queer_theory" title="Queer theory">queer theory</a>, have considered language in their theorizing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Most of these theories have maintained a critical stance on language that calls for a change in the way speakers use their language.
</p><p>One of the most common calls is for gender-neutral language. Many have called attention, however, to the fact that the English language is not inherently sexist in its linguistic system, but the way it is used becomes sexist and gender-neutral language could thus be employed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Other opposed critiques of sexism in language maintain that language is descriptive, rather than prescriptive, and attempts to control it can be fruitless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexism_in_languages_other_than_English">Sexism in languages other than English</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanic_languages" title="Romanic languages">Romanic languages</a> such as <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-90">[90]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-91">[91]</a></sup> may be seen as reinforcing sexism, in that the masculine form is the default. The word "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mademoiselle" title="wikt:mademoiselle">mademoiselle</a>", meaning "<a href="/wiki/Miss" title="Miss">miss</a>", was declared banished from French administrative forms in 2012 by Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Fillon" title="François Fillon">François Fillon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-90">[90]</a></sup> Current pressure calls for the use of the masculine plural pronoun as the default in a mixed-sex group to change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> As for Spanish, Mexico's Ministry of the Interior published a guide on how to reduce the use of sexist language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> speakers have also raised questions about how sexism intersects with grammar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The German language is heavily inflected for gender, number, and case; nearly all nouns denoting the occupations or statuses of human beings are gender-differentiated. For more gender-neutral constructions, gerund nouns are sometimes used instead, as this eliminates the grammatical gender distinction in the plural, and significantly reduces it in the singular. For example, instead of <i>die Studenten</i> ("the men students") or <i>die Studentinnen</i> ("the women students"), one writes <i>die Studierenden</i> ("the [people who are] studying").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> However, this approach introduces an element of ambiguity, because gerund nouns more precisely denote one currently engaged in the activity, rather than one who routinely engages in it as their primary occupation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>, some writers have pointed to sexism inherent in the structure of written characters. For example, the character for man is linked to those for positive qualities like courage and effect while the character for wife is composed of a female part and a broom, considered of low worth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender-specific_pejorative_terms">Gender-specific pejorative terms</span></h3>
<p>Gender-specific pejorative terms intimidate or harm another person because of their gender. Sexism can be expressed in language with negative gender-oriented implications,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disdain" title="Disdain">condescension</a>. For example, one may refer to a female as a "girl" rather than a "woman", implying that they are subordinate or not fully mature. Other examples include obscene language. Some words are offensive to transgender people, including "tranny", "she-male", or "he-she". Intentional <a href="/wiki/Transphobia#Misgendering_and_exclusion" title="Transphobia">misgendering</a> (assigning the wrong gender to someone) and the pronoun "it" are also considered pejorative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills_College_Report_on_Inclusion_of_Transgender_and_Gender_Non-Conforming_Students_Best_Practices,_Assessment_and_Recommendations_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mills_College_Report_on_Inclusion_of_Transgender_and_Gender_Non-Conforming_Students_Best_Practices,_Assessment_and_Recommendations-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-San_Diego_LGBT_Weekly_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-San_Diego_LGBT_Weekly-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occupational_sexism">Occupational sexism</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Occupational_sexism" title="Occupational sexism">Occupational sexism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second-generation_gender_bias" title="Second-generation gender bias">Second-generation gender bias</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%; border-width: 1px; font-size: 80%; background-color: #F9F9F9; margin–top:1.5em;margin-bottom:1.5em;padding:2.0em">
<div class="quotebox-title" style="background-color: #F9F9F9; color: #555555;">"Calling nurses by their first names"</div>
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>The practice of using first names for individuals from a profession that is predominantly female occurs in health care. Physicians are typically referred to using their last name, but nurses are referred to, even by physicians they do not know, by their first name. According to Suzanne Gordon, a typical conversation between a physician and a nurse is: "Hello Jane. I'm Dr. Smith. Would you hand me the patient's chart?"
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="center-aligned" style="">–<i>Nursing Against the Odds: How Health Care Cost Cutting, Media Stereotypes, and Medical Hubris Undermine Nurses and Patient Care</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div><p>
Occupational sexism refers to <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discriminatory</a> practices, statements or actions, based on a person's <a href="/wiki/Sex" title="Sex">sex</a>, occurring in the workplace. One form of occupational sexism is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wage_discrimination" title="Wage discrimination">wage discrimination</a>. In 2008, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a> (OECD) found that while female employment rates have expanded and gender employment and wage gaps have narrowed nearly everywhere, on average women still have 20% less chance to have a job and are paid 17% less than men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd1_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-oecd1-101">[101]</a></sup> The report stated: </p><blockquote><p>[In] many countries, labour market discrimination—i.e. the unequal treatment of equally productive individuals only because they belong to a specific group—is still a crucial factor inflating disparities in employment and the quality of job opportunities [...] Evidence presented in this edition of the <i>Employment Outlook</i> suggests that about 8<span class="nowrap"> </span>percent of the variation in gender employment gaps and 30 percent of the variation in gender wage gaps across OECD countries can be explained by discriminatory practices in the labor market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd1_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-oecd1-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd2_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-oecd2-102">[102]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>It also found that although almost all OECD countries, including the U.S.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> have established anti-discrimination laws, these laws are difficult to enforce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd1_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-oecd1-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Women who enter predominantly male work groups can experience the negative consequences of <a href="/wiki/Tokenism" title="Tokenism">tokenism</a>: performance pressures, social isolation, and role encapsulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoder_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yoder-104">[104]</a></sup> Tokenism could be used to camouflage sexism, to preserve male workers' advantage in the workplace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yoder_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yoder-104">[104]</a></sup> No link exists between the proportion of women working in an organization/company and the improvement of their working conditions. Ignoring sexist issues may exacerbate women's occupational problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/World_Values_Survey" title="World Values Survey">World Values Survey</a></i> of 2005, responders were asked if they thought wage work should be restricted to men only. In Iceland, the percentage that agreed was 3.6%, whereas in Egypt it was 94.9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gap_in_hiring">Gap in hiring</span></h3>
<p>Research has repeatedly shown that mothers in the United States are less likely to be hired than equally qualified fathers and if hired, receive a lower salary than male applicants with children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Folbre_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Folbre-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodman_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cahn_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cahn-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Correll_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Correll-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cornell_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Bundling_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This claim has too many footnotes for reading to be smooth. (December 2021)">excessive citations</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>One study found that female applicants were favored; however, its results have been met with skepticism from other researchers, since it contradicts most other studies on the issue. Joan C. Williams, a distinguished professor at the University of California's Hastings College of Law, raised issues with its methodology, pointing out that the fictional female candidates it used were unusually well-qualified. Studies using more moderately qualified graduate students have found that male students are much more likely to be hired, offered better salaries, and offered mentorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Europe, studies based on field experiments in the labor market, provide evidence for no severe levels of discrimination based on female gender. However, unequal treatment is still measured in particular situations, for instance, when candidates apply for positions at a higher functional level in Belgium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (October 2015)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> when they apply at their fertile ages in France,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (October 2015)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> and when they apply for male-dominated occupations in Austria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earnings_gap">Earnings gap</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gender_pay_gap" title="Gender pay gap">Gender pay gap</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png"><img alt="Bar graph showing the gender pay gap in European countries" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png/300px-EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png/450px-EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png/600px-EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gender pay gap in average gross hourly earnings according to <a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">Eurostat</a> 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC_stats_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-EC_stats-118">[118]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Studies have concluded that on average women earn lower wages than men worldwide. Some people argue that this results from widespread gender discrimination in the workplace. Others argue that the wage gap results from different choices by men and women, such as women placing more value than men on having children, and men being more likely than women to choose careers in high paying fields such as business, engineering, and technology.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">Eurostat</a> found a persistent, average gender pay gap of 27.5% in the 27 <a href="/wiki/Member_state_of_the_European_Union" title="Member state of the European Union">EU member states</a> in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EC_stats_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-EC_stats-118">[118]</a></sup> Similarly, the OECD found that female full-time employees earned 27% less than their male counterparts in OECD countries in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd1_101-3"><a href="#cite_note-oecd1-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd2_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-oecd2-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the female-to-male earnings ratio was 0.77 in 2009; female full-time, year-round (FTYR) workers earned 77% as much as male FTYR workers. Women's earnings relative to men's fell from 1960 to 1980 (56.7–54.2%), rose rapidly from 1980 to 1990 (54.2–67.6%), leveled off from 1990 to 2000 (67.6–71.2%) and rose from 2000 to 2009 (71.2–77.0%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census_2009_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-census_2009-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> As of the late 2010s, it has decreased back to around 1990 to 2000 levels (68.6-71.1%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> When the first <a href="/wiki/Equal_Pay_Act_of_1963" title="Equal Pay Act of 1963">Equal Pay Act</a> was passed in 1963, female full-time workers earned 48.9% as much as male full-time workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census_2009_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-census_2009-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research conducted in Czechia and Slovakia shows that, even after the governments passed anti-discrimination legislation, two thirds of the gender gap in wages remained unexplained and segregation continued to "represent a major source of the gap".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>The gender gap can also vary across-occupation and within occupation. In Taiwan, for example, studies show how the bulk of gender wage discrepancies occur within-occupation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In Russia, research shows that the gender wage gap is distributed unevenly across income levels, and that it mainly occurs at the lower end of income distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-125">[125]</a></sup> The research also found that "wage arrears and payment in-kind attenuated wage discrimination, particularly amongst the lowest paid workers, suggesting that Russian enterprise managers assigned lowest importance to equity considerations when allocating these forms of payment".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>The gender pay gap has been attributed to differences in personal and workplace characteristics between men and women (such as education, hours worked and occupation), innate behavioral and biological differences between men and women and discrimination in the labor market (such as gender stereotypes and customer and employer bias). Women take significantly more time off to raise children than men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> In certain countries such as South Korea, it has also been a long-established practice to lay-off female employees upon marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> A study by Professor <a href="/wiki/Linda_C._Babcock" title="Linda C. Babcock">Linda C. Babcock</a> in her book <i>Women Don't Ask</i> shows that men are eight times more likely to ask for a pay raise, suggesting that pay inequality may be partly a result of behavioral differences between the sexes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> However, studies generally find that a portion of the gender pay gap remains unexplained after accounting for factors assumed to influence earnings; the unexplained portion of the wage gap is attributed to gender discrimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimates of the discriminatory component of the gender pay gap vary. The OECD estimated that approximately 30% of the gender pay gap across OECD countries is because of discrimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd1_101-4"><a href="#cite_note-oecd1-101">[101]</a></sup> Australian research shows that discrimination accounts for approximately 60% of the wage differential between men and women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natsem_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-natsem-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Male%E2%80%93female_income_disparity_in_the_United_States#Explaining_the_gender_pay_gap" title="Male–female income disparity in the United States">Studies examining the gender pay gap in the United States</a> show that a much of the wage differential remains unexplained, after controlling for factors affecting pay. One study of college graduates found that the portion of the pay gap unexplained after all other factors are taken into account is five percent one year after graduating and 12% a decade after graduation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-denverpost.com_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-denverpost.com-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> A study by the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_of_University_Women" title="American Association of University Women">American Association of University Women</a> found that women graduates in the United States are paid less than men doing the same work and majoring in the same field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_gender_pay_gap,_by_sex,_race-ethnicity-2009.png"><img alt="Graph showing weekly earnings by various categories" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/US_gender_pay_gap%2C_by_sex%2C_race-ethnicity-2009.png/300px-US_gender_pay_gap%2C_by_sex%2C_race-ethnicity-2009.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/US_gender_pay_gap%2C_by_sex%2C_race-ethnicity-2009.png/450px-US_gender_pay_gap%2C_by_sex%2C_race-ethnicity-2009.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/US_gender_pay_gap%2C_by_sex%2C_race-ethnicity-2009.png/600px-US_gender_pay_gap%2C_by_sex%2C_race-ethnicity-2009.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_gender_pay_gap,_by_sex,_race-ethnicity-2009.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Median weekly earnings of full-time wage and salary workers, by sex, race, and ethnicity, U.S., 2009<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bls_2009_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-bls_2009-137">[137]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Wage discrimination is theorized as contradicting the economic concept of <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply and demand</a>, which states that if a good or service (in this case, labor) is in demand and has value it will find its price in the market. If a worker offered equal value for less pay, supply and demand would indicate a greater demand for lower-paid workers. If a business hired lower-wage workers for the same work, it would lower its costs and enjoy a <a href="/wiki/Competitive_advantage" title="Competitive advantage">competitive advantage</a>. According to supply and demand, if women offered equal value demand (and wages) should rise since they offer a better price (lower wages) for their service than men do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research at Cornell University and elsewhere indicates that mothers in the United States are less likely to be hired than equally qualified fathers and, if hired, receive a lower salary than male applicants with children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Folbre_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Folbre-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodman_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cahn_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cahn-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Young-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Correll_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Correll-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cornell_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-112">[112]</a></sup> The OECD found that "a significant impact of children on women's pay is generally found in the United Kingdom and the United States".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd.org_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-oecd.org-139">[139]</a></sup> Fathers earn <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USD" title="USD">$</a>7,500 more, on average, than men without children do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-womensmedia.com_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-womensmedia.com-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is research to suggest that the gender wage gap leads to big losses for the economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_for_wage_discrimination"><span class="anchor" id="Proposed_Explanations"></span> Causes for wage discrimination</span></h4>
<p>The non-adjusted gender pay gap (the difference without taking into account differences in working hours, occupations, education and work experience) is not itself a measure of discrimination. Rather, it combines differences in the average pay of women and men to serve as a barometer of comparison. Differences in pay are caused by:
</p>
<ul><li>occupational segregation (with more men in higher paid industries and women in lower paid industries),</li>
<li>vertical segregation (fewer women in senior, and hence better paying positions),</li>
<li>ineffective equal pay legislation,</li>
<li>women's overall paid working hours, and</li>
<li>barriers to entry into the labor market (such as education level and single parenting rate).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-142">[142]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Some variables that help explain the non-adjusted gender pay gap include economic activity, working time, and job tenure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto3_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-142">[142]</a></sup> Gender-specific factors, including gender differences in qualifications and discrimination, overall wage structure, and the differences in remuneration across industry sectors all influence the gender pay gap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">Eurostat</a> estimated in 2016 that after allowing for average characteristics of men and women, women still earn 11.5% less than men. Since this estimate accounts for average differences between men and women, it is an estimation of the <i>unexplained</i> gender pay gap<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glass_ceiling_effect">Glass ceiling effect</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Glass_ceiling" title="Glass ceiling">Glass ceiling</a></div>
<p>"The popular notion of glass ceiling effects implies that gender (or other) disadvantages are stronger at the top of the hierarchy than at lower levels and that these disadvantages become worse later in a person's career."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, women account for 52% of the overall labor force, but make up only three percent of corporate CEOs and top executives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_A_2011_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-David_A_2011-146">[146]</a></sup> Some researchers see the root cause of this situation in the tacit discrimination based on gender, conducted by current top executives and corporate directors (primarily male), and "the historic absence of women in top positions", which "may lead to <a href="/wiki/Hysteresis" title="Hysteresis">hysteresis</a>, preventing women from accessing powerful, male-dominated professional networks, or same-sex mentors".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_A_2011_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-David_A_2011-146">[146]</a></sup> The glass ceiling effect is noted as being especially persistent for women of color. According to a report, "women of colour perceive a 'concrete ceiling' and not simply a glass ceiling".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_A_2011_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-David_A_2011-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the economics profession, it has been observed that women are more inclined than men to dedicate their time to teaching and service. Since continuous research work is crucial for promotion, "the cumulative effect of small, contemporaneous differences in research orientation could generate the observed significant gender difference in promotion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> In the high-tech industry, research shows that, regardless of the intra-firm changes, "extra-organizational pressures will likely contribute to continued gender stratification as firms upgrade, leading to the potential masculinization of skilled high-tech work".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United Nations asserts that "progress in bringing women into leadership and decision making positions around the world remains far too slow".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN-IWD2006_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN-IWD2006-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_remedies">Potential remedies</span></h4>
<p>Research by David Matsa and Amalia Miller suggests that a remedy to the glass ceiling could be increasing the number of women on corporate boards, which could lead to increases in the number of women working in top management positions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_A_2011_146-3"><a href="#cite_note-David_A_2011-146">[146]</a></sup> The same research suggests that this could also result in a "feedback cycle in which the presence of more female managers increases the qualified pool of potential female board members (for the companies they manage, as well as other companies), leading to greater female board membership and then further increases in female executives".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN-IWD2006_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN-IWD2006-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weight-based_sexism">Weight-based sexism</span></h3>
<p>A 2009 study found that being overweight harms women's career advancement, but presents no barrier for men. <a href="/wiki/Overweight" title="Overweight">Overweight</a> women were significantly underrepresented among company bosses, making up between five and 22% of female CEOs. However, the proportion of overweight male CEOs was between 45% and 61%, over-representing overweight men. On the other hand, approximately five percent of CEOs were obese among both genders. The author of the study stated that the results suggest that "the '<a href="/wiki/Glass_ceiling" title="Glass ceiling">glass ceiling</a> effect' on women's advancement may reflect not only general negative stereotypes about the competencies of women but also weight bias that results in the application of stricter appearance standards to women."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
